SUBJECT:
San Mateo Street Tree Master Plan - Update
RECOMMENDATION:
Review and comment on the proposed revisions to the Street Tree Master Plan.
BACKGROUND:
The City of San Mateo maintains 23,000 public space trees along City streets. Each city block has an assigned tree species 
per the Street Tree Master Plan (STMP), most recently updated in 2011. Currently, the STMP tree pallet is heavily weighted 
towards temperate zone (i.e. cold tolerant) water-loving trees. These tree species were never well adapted to California's 
drought-based climate without substantial supplemental irrigation. Climate change exacerbates this incompatibility with 
hotter temperatures, less predictable rainfall, and an absence of cold temperatures, which some of these tree species 
need to thrive. The Bay Area climate is rapidly becoming subtropical, and more like Southern California. The water loving 
trees commonly planted in San Mateo are almost totally absent in Southern California, as they simply don't survive the hot 
and dry conditions without copious amount of irrigation. Some of the familiar species that are struggling along San 
Mateo’s streets include southern magnolia, sycamore, sweetgum, and various species of maple.
San Mateo needs a revised tree palette that can perform well in the face of climate extremes. Such species can be found in 
places that experience the range of temperatures and precipitation that the City will face in the future. Correcting the 
current ecological misalignment is critical to ensuring a beneficial and equitable urban forest in San Mateo over the coming 
decades of climate extremes and water restriction. 
Over the past six months, the Managing Arborist has been working closely with consultant Dave Muffly (Oaktopia, LLC) to 
re-develop the City's Street Tree Master Plan by selecting tree species that will perform well in the face of our warming 
and drying climate. All blocks have been reassigned a climate-ready tree species, prioritizing the following characteristics: 
• Proven drought tolerance and longevity. 
• Representative of broad genetic diversity. 
• Ability to withstand changing climate patterns. 
• Minimal debris production. 
• High resistance to pests and disease. 
• Low root damage potential. 
• Failure-resistant branch structure. 
• Site-appropriate canopy size and shape. 
• Nursery availability. 
Any change in appearance to San Mateo’s streetscape will occur very slowly. No trees will be removed to make way for 
6 of 157

--- page 7 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO
new species. Trees will continue to be removed only according to the current criteria—poor condition, death, high risk to 
the public, or severe infrastructure conflict. Parks manages a street tree inventory of 23,000 trees and replaces 
approximately 200 trees annually, so the species composition of our street trees will only change by about 1% per year.
The Managing Arborist and Mr. Muffly are still revising the final copy and layout of the Master Plan and hope to distribute 
it to the Commission before the May meeting. The near-final version of the Master Plan is Attachment 1 to this report.

Introduction 
Since the widespread planting of urban trees began in California roughly a century ago, there 
has been a strong preference for species with particular aesthetic characteristics, especially 
flowers and fall color. These trees have come from all over world, except, interestingly, 
California itself. Many if not most of these trees have come from places with greater yearly 
precipitation than San Mateo, and much different precipitation timing. These water-biased 
trees have survived our droughty climate with the assistance of supplemental irrigation. 
 
With the return of serious drought in California since the 1970s, accompanied by surging 
population growth and water demand, our water-loving urban tree species are under 
unprecedented pressure. Beloved trees like southern magnolia are stressed and dying 
everywhere you look. And this is only the beginning. As we look toward our future climate, 
where do we look for the trees that support our physical and mental wellbeing?  
 
To orient ourselves, we start with a basic question. What characteristics define a successful 
urban street tree for San Mateo in the 21st century? 
 
Essential Characteristics: 
Proven drought tolerance and longevity, native as appropriate. 
Representative of broad genetic diversity. 
Ability to withstand changing climate patterns. 
Minimal debris production. 
High resistance to pests and disease. 
Low root damage potential. 
Failure-resistant branch structure. 
Site-appropriate canopy size and shape. 
Nursery availability. 
 
Preferred Characteristics: 
Evergreen leaf habit. 
Colorful flowers, new growth, or dormant season coloration. 
High habitat value. 
Visual cohesion with existing urban forest. 
 
Drought Tolerance and Longevity 
 Drought is the norm in California. San Mateo has a Mediterranean climate, defined by a long 
 rainless dry season in the warmest part of the year (regular summer drought), with rain 
 falling primarily during the coolest part of the year. This is a difficult climate for many trees 
 from around the world due to the yearly summer drought. Most of Earth's trees grow in 
 areas with greater precipitation than San Mateo, and, critically, with rain falling during the 
 warm growing season. Coastal Californians have historically grown many summer-rain trees, 
 but a series of increasingly severe droughts since the 1970s have demonstrated that further 
 investment in water-needy tree species is simply unwise. 
10 of 157

--- page 11 ---
Page 4 of 128 
 
 
While many tree species from global arid zones have been tried in California, the vast 
diversity of life on Earth means that many potentially useful drought-adapted trees have 
NOT been grown here. This creates lots of opportunity for the thoughtful entrepreneur. 
Horticultural experiments have been implemented in recent decades by the University of 
California, Stanford University, the US Forest Service, and others, in preparation for the 
sweeping effects of climate change. The results of these experimental plantings are becoming 
clear, and successful new drought tolerant trees are being identified and grown for trial use 
throughout the state.  
 
The trees specified in the Street Tree Master Plan are a mix of long-proven species, and 
strongly performing new entries. The list represents a broad diversity of species, many of 
which are new to the species-rich but drought intolerant assortment of trees growing along 
the streets of San Mateo. 
 
Broad Genetic Diversity 
Like a wise investor facing a period of economic uncertainty, a wise urban forest manager 
adapting to climate change chooses to diversify their portfolio. Only in this case, the 
portfolio is comprised of trees. Diversifying urban forests is a widely discussed concept, but 
discussions typically overlook the context that defines nursery tree adaptation and 
availability. The most common mistake when crafting street tree lists is emphasizing quantity 
of species and genera, over quality and suitability. Bay Area municipal tree lists are full of 
species not adapted to our arid climate without supplemental irrigation. Deciduous, fall color 
species from wet summer climates fill the lists, and such species often stand stunted and 
dying along our streets.  
 
Because of California's aridity, our selection of adapted trees is a fraction of those available 
in the wet summer eastern United States, where the bulk of America's trees are planted in 
the many dense cities. There are only a limited number of tree genera that are truly adapted 
to arid climates like California, and only certain species of those genera have the needed 
drought adaptations. Oaks (Quercus), pines (Pinus), eucalypts (Eucalyptus), and a few others 
like olive (Olea), and acacia (Acacia), provide the majority of all arid adapted tree species. But 
many pine trees shed large cones, making the trees inappropriate to the urban context. Many 
eucalypts grow to huge sizes, making them poor urban tree selections. 
 
Historically, oaks constituted the majority of all native trees in San Mateo. Pines, eucalypts, 
olives, acacias and others were added later. By comparison to other arid adapted genera, oaks 
produce seeds which are moderately smaller in size, and oak trees typically have a strong and 
robust structure. Oaks include numerous arid adapted species, many of which are 
ecologically related to California native species. Oaks of all kinds support biodiversity. The 
oak genus has a deep roster of adapted species, while other genera, maples (Acer) for 
example, offer only a few marginal candidates. Oaks give the arid zone urban tree planter 
many more usable choices than other arid zone tree genera. 
11 of 157

--- page 12 ---
Page 5 of 128 
 
Historically, San Mateo hosted the oak subgenus Lobatae, the black oaks, in the form of coast 
live oak (Quercus agrifolia). San Mateo has also hosted the oak subgenus Quercus, the white 
oaks, in the form of valley oak (Quercus lobata). Although oaks are taxonomically considered a 
single genus, not all oak trees are reproductively compatible, and therefore oaks are broken 
up into several subgenera.  
 
These subgenera often have different pest and disease susceptibilities and resistances, as 
exemplified by the fact that the Sudden Oak Death syndrome in coastal California afflicts 
the black oak subgenera, leaving the white oak subgenera untouched. Reproductive 
incompatibility between oak subgenera, in concert with these varying disease and pest 
susceptibilities, argues for oaks to be considered something other than a monolithic plant 
genus, at least in the context of practical urban forestry in historically oak-dominated 
landscapes.  
 
In addition to some new representatives of the black and white oak subgenera historically 
present in San Mateo, the updated Street Tree Master Plan incorporates intermediate oaks 
(Protobalanus), and oaks from the Turkey oak group (Cerris). While these two subgenera were 
not previously represented in the flora of San Mateo, each contain arid adapted and urban 
friendly species, including at least one California native species. Oaks from these subgenera 
possess unique adaptations and resistances, increasing overall urban forest resiliency. 
 
A Question of Nativity 
In recent decades, interest in, and advocacy for, California native plants has skyrocketed. But 
it is important to remember that no California native plant evolved in a city, and an urban 
growing environment is radically different than anything found in nature. In the city, soils 
are shifted, mixed with waste, and compacted; water is applied in amounts and timing far 
from what the native environment offers; historical underground water flows are altered and 
often contaminated; pollution is ever-present, altering the gases which plants respire, and 
depositing a variety of sometimes toxic substances onto their leaves; relationships with 
native pests and pest predators are altered; and patterns of sunlight and ultraviolet light are 
transformed by pavement and buildings.  
 
Then there's the question of where any particular California native plant grows in nature, and 
the vital differences between its original microclimate and the environment in which the 
native plant is proposed for planting. People may assume that if a plant is native to 
California, it will perform well anywhere in California. But with a bewildering array of 
microclimates in California, nothing could be further from the truth. Remembering that 
California microclimates range from full desert to temperate rainforest to glacial alpine to 
classic Mediterranean savannah helps people understand why saying that a plant is from 
California says virtually nothing about the conditions in which that plant will grow.  
 
12 of 157

--- page 13 ---
Page 6 of 128 
 
Today, native tree lists are circulated along with requests to plant more “native” trees in the 
public right of way. This makes it important to understand the fallacies implicit in commonly 
circulated native tree lists.  
 
First, many of the plants on these lists are shrubs rather than trees. People are often shocked 
by how few real native California trees can be grown in California cities, and the list makers 
don't seem to consistently respect urban plant realities. Because of their small mature size, 
most native shrubs have highly limited application to public right-of-way plantings. Trees in 
the right-of-way must be pruned with substantial clearances, 8 feet or more for people, 15 
feet or more when clearing for vehicular traffic. The mature height of a plant needs to be 
above the minimum clearance height to function as a tree.  
 
Second, many of the plants on these lists are native to the mountains of California and the 
Bay Area, not the low-elevation valley areas where most of California's population lives. The 
soils are very different in the mountains than at lower elevations, with much slower drainage 
in the valleys where clay is common. Many California native trees require fast soil drainage 
that is not available in low elevation areas, especially with typical urban soil impacts. There 
are also major differences in rainfall between mountains and lowlands, with mountain 
locations receiving 3 to 5 times as much rainfall by comparison to lower elevation areas. 
These are huge ecological differences that lead to big differences in plant adaptation.  
 
Ability to Withstand Changing Climate Conditions 
As the earth warms, climate conditions are moving from the equator toward the poles. In 
the Northern Hemisphere, this means, in general, climate conditions found to the south are 
headed north. This movement suggests that species adapted for San Mateo’s future may be 
found, generally, to the south. For the Bay Area, the minimum expectation is climate 
conditions currently found in southern California will be present by mid-to-late century. 
Expectations more in line with the faster observed pace of climate change suggest those 
from Mexico are headed to the Bay Area, especially Baja and northwestern to central 
Mexico.  
 
Interestingly, northwestern Mexico is a global biodiversity hotspot for many organisms, 
including oak and pine trees. Just that portion of Mexico has several times as many oak 
species as all of California combined. Initial plantings of sample oak species from these areas 
of Mexico into urban California have been highly successful, and several of these species are 
being introduced into the California nursery industry. The silverleaf oak (Quercus hypoleucoides), 
the netleaf oak (Quercus rugosa), and the Mexican blue oak (Quercus oblongifolia) are all being 
sold out of California nurseries as quickly as they can be grown. These trees are becoming a 
source of beauty and resiliency for California cities, from San Diego to Santa Rosa to 
Sacramento. This trend toward Mexican oaks for urban California is likely to continue. 
The adoption of southerly species from Mexico may confuse people, as there are a variety of 
proposals regarding climate change tree selection that claim strong scientific backing. One of 
these proposals is to assert that California native plants are best adapted to current and 
13 of 157

--- page 14 ---
Page 7 of 128 
 
future climate flux, but this assertion is proving out of step with modern climate science. 
Published scientific projections of future geographical range of California trees1 show 
movements of hundreds to thousands of miles, often right out of California. These 
projections suggest we need to look for trees roughly as far south to compensate. And the 
wider the geographical range those trees occupy, the better. 
 
A decade ago, one of the top biological scientists at the University of California made a wise 
prediction about the future of plants in the era of climate change. Given the extremes that 
accompany climate change, the future belongs to generalist organisms (those which occur 
over a large geographical area and in a wide range of microclimates), rather than specialist 
organisms (those which grow only in very specific and limited conditions). Put simply, to 
adapt to climate change, we need trees that are already genetically evolved to survive a wide 
range of climate conditions, to handle the inevitable extreme climate flux.  
 
Only a small proportion of California native plants are generalists with a wide area of 
adaptation. The density and variety of microclimates in California has favored the evolution 
of native plants that are often specialist organisms, dependent on microclimates that occur in 
limited geographical areas. Climate change is making a quick mess of these microclimates 
and their denizens. Future plant range projection science shows substantial geographic 
displacement of all native trees, sometimes in excess of 1,000 miles. Looking a similar 
distance south of the Bay Area, there are some native trees that can serve as urban species.  
Engelman oak (Quercus engelmannii), comes from southern California (the very climate zones 
headed to the Bay Area) is already used as a street tree in some California cites, especially 
Pasadena. Engelman oak is proving to grow wonderfully in real world Bay Area landscape 
conditions too. Test plantings at Stanford University and East Palo Alto are thriving. 
Engelman oak is a great example of the successful search for twenty-first century trees for 
California cities, using the "look south" approach.  
 
An expansion of the "look south" approach is to look for trees which have a large 
geographical range to the south containing multiple microclimates. The beautiful silverleaf 
oak (Quercus hypoleucoides), is a good example, with a native range stretching from central 
Mexico all the way to the American Southwest. Adding to the attraction of silverleaf oak is 
the fact that they have been successfully grown nearly 1,000 miles north of their native 
range, in Portland, Oregon. Its wide geographical range of adaptation, combined with highly 
favorable early growth in coastal California cities, give silverleaf oak a place on the Street 
Tree Master Plan. 
 
Another approach to finding candidate urban species is observational. Here we look for 
trees that grow well in a wide variety of locations and situations in urban California, and 
similar conditions to the south. A great example of this is the southern live oak (Quercus 
virginiana), which has been grown successfully all over urban California. It grows natively 
from Florida to Texas, where it is among the most commonly planted urban trees, and can 
 
1 http://charcoal.cnre.vt.edu/climate/species/ 
14 of 157

--- page 15 ---
Page 8 of 128 
 
be found in the desert cities of Arizona. This is a remarkable range of climate tolerance, 
exactly what is needed when facing a climatically chaotic future. 
 
The performance-based approach to finding good candidate urban trees must be used 
carefully. These judgements need to be based in the observation of large populations of the 
tree in question, ideally over an extended period. It is easy for an observer to see a tree that 
is growing well in a single location, and to assume it will grow well everywhere. However, 
there is wide variation in plant performance based on specific, and often difficult to 
ascertain, site conditions. 
  
As we consider the impacts of climate change, we need to plan for sea level rise. San Mateo 
neighborhoods bordering the San Francisco Bay, and those sitting at or below sea level, will 
be impacted by rising waters and tidal flooding events. Salt, an enemy of plant tissues, will be 
introduced into the soils and groundwater closest to the Bay. In anticipation, salt-tolerant 
tree species have been selected for planting sites near the Bay. A first-choice tree in these 
locations is the versatile silver linden (Tilia tomentosa), the tree planted by the Port of New 
York in all tidally flooded locations. Silver linden trees are already thriving in extensive near-
bay plantings in nearby East Palo Alto and East Menlo Park. As we move away from the 
Bay, we introduce street trees with a lesser but still substantial tolerance for salt, including 
olive (Olea europaea), southern live oak (Quercus virginiana), Trojan oak (Quercus trojana), along 
with various Eucalypts and Corymbia species. 
 
High Resistance to Pests and Disease 
One of the key strategies to long term urban forest resilience is to plant species that resist all 
common local pests and diseases. AS an example, several common street tree species in San 
Mateo have proven highly susceptible to aphids, including holly oak (Quercus ilex), Chinese 
hackberry (Celtis sinensis), and the popular little leaf linden (Tilia cordata). Aphids secrete a 
honeydew substance, and pavement under these species typically remains sticky for the 
lifespan of the tree. For the San Mateo tree list, little leaf linden has been replaced by the 
silver linden (Tilia tomentosa), proven to strongly resist aphid infestation in Bay Area 
plantings. Holly oak is replaced by southern live oak (Quercus virginiana) and other evergreen 
oaks, while Chinese hackberry is replaced by a variety of pest and disease resistant species. 
 
Minimal Debris Production 
Some of the most common complaints about urban trees are related to excessive litter 
production. It's unpleasant to walk through a layer of sticky insect honeydew on the 
pavement beneath a tree, and large acorns can create a tripping hazard. Since trees are by 
nature shedding organisms, the low debris production requirement reduces the urban tree 
palette, particularly the appropriateness of native trees. For example, the native valley oak 
(Quercus lobata), not only grows an enormous, pavement-displacing root flare, but many drop 
large acorns with regularity. As another example, Chinese hackberry (Celtis sinensis), was 
promoted as an ideal urban tree in the 1980s and 90s, and many were planted. They are easy 
15 of 157

--- page 16 ---
Page 9 of 128 
 
to recognize along the streets of San Mateo today, because of the omnipresent circle of 
sticky aphid honeydew directly under their canopy. 
 
In contrast to their dominance in the historical San Mateo landscape, the number of oaks 
used in the new Street Tree Master Plan is rather modest. One of the primary reasons for 
this modesty is acorn production - crops of large acorns can cause both physical and legal 
problems when dropped on the public right-of-way. To help address this issue, several 
tactics have been used. Some oaks have surprisingly tiny acorns. These include some lovely 
and well-adapted new species like the silverleaf oak (Quercus hypoleucoides), and the Mexican 
red oak (Quercus canbyi). Heavier acorn producers, like the otherwise superb netleaf oak 
(Quercus rugosa), are used sparingly, and overwhelmingly in areas where acorn fall is of little 
concern, in places like medians and adjacent to freeway sound walls. 
 
Low Root Damage Potential 
One of the largest costs associated with the long-term maintenance of city trees is the 
necessity to cut roots and replace concrete where sidewalks heave and displace critical 
infrastructure. To ensure the financial sustainability of the San Mateo urban forest, trees 
should be chosen to keep the pavement flat. Luckily, drought tolerant trees typically favor a 
deep rooting strategy to gain access to deep sources of year-round water. Trees from wet 
summer climates often favor the extensive and shallow rooting that shoulders infrastructure 
aside. California pavement bullies like sweetgum (Liquidambar styraciflua), and the southern 
magnolia (Magnolia grandiflora), are known for their lack of drought tolerance, and both have 
died in large numbers in recent decades with growing water conservation and drought. But 
before their decline, these trees were causing huge pavement repair bills in nearly every 
municipality in California. And then they died of drought, highlighting the reality they were 
always a compromised choice. Here again, we see the beneficial synergy between critical tree 
characteristics, in this case drought tolerance and deep rooting. 
 
Failure-Resistant Branch Structure 
That trees should have strong branching structure is a rather obvious requirement of 
successful urban trees. No one wants to read reports of injury or even death, and damage 
payments can strain municipal budgets. For the purposes of the new San Mateo tree list, we 
consciously choose trees with a successful track record of stability in Bay Area cities. Some 
of our small tree selections like olive (Olea europaea 'Swan Hill') and crape myrtle (Lagerstroemis 
sp.) have proven virtually indestructible structurally, while also creating a tree structure with 
a feeling of age and character.  
 
Site Appropriate Canopy Size and Shape 
Another of the greatest costs in the long-term maintenance of California's urban forests is 
what is known as line clearing, the regular cutting of trees to keep them away from energized 
power lines. This practice has skyrocketed in importance in recent years, as tree branches 
16 of 157

--- page 17 ---
Page 10 of 128 
 
blown into energized power lines have caused devastating fires and billions of dollars in 
losses throughout California.  
 
Over the last century, it has been common practice to plant the same tree species on both 
sides of a given street, regardless of whether one side had power lines or not. This practice 
requires constant and costly tree pruning to keep energy flowing safely. Respecting this 
reality, the new Street Tree Master Plan often includes an appropriate large canopy tree 
species on the non-power line side of the street, while using one of our limited palette of 
durable smaller trees under the power lines themselves.  This kind of adaptive precision 
creates maximum long term financial and ecological sustainability. 
 
The growth habit of the trees needs to be generally upright in branch angle to avoid creating 
a major long term pruning cost. Trees with a pendulous or horizontal growth habit should 
generally be avoided.  
 
Nursery Availability 
All trees on the latest Master Plan are already in production at area nurseries. As an added 
precaution, the San Mateo tree list includes a selection of acceptable substitutes should the 
tree specified in the plan be unavailable at any given time. 
 
Preference for Evergreen Trees 
San Mateo’s current street tree population is tilted toward deciduous trees. This composition 
of is out of step with both ecological history and scientific predictions of future climate. San 
Mateo, like all of coastal California, is historically biased toward evergreen trees, both 
broadleaf and conifer. Deciduousness largely evolved to prevent water-filled leaf cells from 
bursting in freezing temperatures. Such conditions are quickly disappearing, if not gone 
already. From its historical evergreen baseline, San Mateo is facing a future climate even 
more selective of evergreen tree species.  
 
Because the proportion of evergreen to deciduous tree numbers will change very slowly, new 
tree plantings in San Mateo should favor evergreen species whenever possible for the 
foreseeable future. Deciduous trees still have a place in the urban forest, but on a generalized 
basis and with notable exceptions, evergreen trees are strikingly more drought tolerant than 
deciduous species.  
 
Balancing the investment in evergreen species are homeowner solar access considerations 
which favor deciduous trees. Having a large evergreen tree south and west of a residence 
blocks out substantial winter sun, which some homeowners reasonably consider an 
impediment to critical household solar access. With this consideration in mind, streets with a 
southerly exposure receive the best drought adapted deciduous trees available, often in 
conjunction with an evergreen tree on the opposite side of the street. 
 
17 of 157

--- page 18 ---
Page 11 of 128 
 
Preference for colorful flowers, attractive new growth, or vivid dormant 
season coloration 
Bringing some color to the urban forest is a welcome addition to resident experience. 
However, flowers can sometimes produce troublesome levels of debris, so flowering trees 
must be used with careful consideration. Trees with flowers conspicuous to humans are 
relatively rare in the natural world, especially in the arid subtropics which are a key source of 
trees for the future Bay Area. Several kinds of flowering trees are used in the new Street Tree 
Master Plan, including crape myrtles (Lagerstroemia sp.), subtropical flame trees (Koelreuteria 
elegans), and various Corymbia species. Fall color entries include Compton oak (Quercus x 
comptoniae), tree form Gambel oak (Quercus gambelii 'Mount Lemmon'), the southwestern 
chinkapin oak (Quercus muehlenbergii var. brayi), and, for mid-winter color, the Mexican red oak 
(Quercus canbyi). Trees with particularly attractive new growth include the netleaf oak (Quercus 
rugosa), and the silverleaf oak (Quercus hypoleuccoides). 
 
Preference for trees with high habitat value 
We live in a moment of vast pressure on the planet's biodiversity, with habitats shifting and 
extinctions becoming commonplace. Many people concerned with biodiversity consider the 
selection of local native plants to be of utmost importance, but they are forgetting that 
climate zones are rapidly shifting substantially. Scientific research shows much of life 
planetwide shifting location away from the equator, or uphill, to match their evolved climate. 
Trees, especially oaks, underpin vast amounts of biodiversity. From microfauna to macro, 
countless organisms favor oaks for food and shelter. By planting these fundamental 
ecosystem trees, we may be able to create homes for refugee organisms shifting range to 
match changes in climate. Trees with potentially high habitat value include the California 
native Engelman oak (Quercus engelmannii), and the very closely related Mexican blue oak 
(Quercus oblongifolia), the California native island oak (Quercus tomentella), and the southwestern 
native Chisos red oak (Quercus gravesii), the closest relative to the California native black oak 
group, including the local native coast live oak (Quercus agrifolia). 
 
 
Preference for trees with a characterful structure 
When we think of trees with structural character, we often think of oaks. These classics 
motifs can be found throughout the iconography of the human world, a testament to their 
fundamental resonance with the human psyche. This shows people like trees with distinctive 
character, suggesting great age, rather than generic look-alike green tree “blobs”. Examples 
of trees which pack a lot of character into a small space in a short span of years includes:  
olive trees (Olea sp.), crape myrtles (Lagerstroemia sp.), oaks like the Trojan oak (Quercus 
trojana), and Chisos red oaks (Quercus gravesii), and even some of the ultra-durable small 
Eucalyptus trees, like Eucalyptus spathulata. 
 
 
18 of 157

--- page 19 ---
Page 12 of 128 
 
Preference for trees that provide visual cohesion with the existing 
urban forest 
In the interest of completeness, efforts have been made to select street trees that are in 
harmony with pre-existing urban forest elements. These efforts took a variety of forms. For 
example, adding evergreen trees to a street already planted with deciduous fall color trees 
gives the street the feel of mixed woodlands, matching the gestalt of native woodlands. 
Aesthetically, evergreens make a great background for colorful deciduous trees, and give a 
sense of life and greenery during the warm winter months.  
 
Sometimes maintaining visual cohesion requires compromises to maintain existing uses. For 
example, many streets in San Mateo lie on hillsides which afford lovely views of the Bay and 
surrounding mountains. Many of these streets have few trees, as residents may prioritize 
view over tree cover with views being important to property values. In such places large 
trees could compete with scenic views, so modest size trees have been selected for those 
sites. Many of these streets are specified for olive trees, as there are few trees more iconic 
than an olive on a Mediterranean hillside. 
 
A note about Eucalyptus trees 
Many Eucalyptus species have become naturalized in California since their introduction about 
150 years ago. While there are over 600 species ofEucalyptus, the entire genus suffers a 
reputation problem due to the characteristics of one member, the blue gum (E. globulus). The 
enormous size, potential invasiveness, heavy litter production, and pyrophytic nature render 
the blue gum as inappropriate for San Mateo streets. However, some Eucalyptus and related 
genera of Lophostemon, Angophora, and Corymbia, have proven to behave better than Eucalyptus 
globulus under the right conditions. These species grow smaller than blue gum and remain an 
important part of the California landscapes, especially in southern California. Eucalyptus and 
their related genera possess traits uniquely adapted to our changing climate. Rejecting several 
genera of trees due to the behavior of the blue gum is not appropriate or advised. 
Eucalyptus and their related genera excel in salty soils where many other species are intolerant 
and are commonly seen around the edges of the San Francisco Bay. Many of these locations 
where Eucalypts are successful no California native tree can survive.  
Mallees are small trees related to Eucalyptus and possess remarkable drought and salt 
tolerances. Swamp mallee Eucalyptus spathulata which only grows to forty feet tall is the best 
known of the mallees in California. The tree has been used successfully in landscapes 
throughout the state, and particularly in locations adjacent to the San Francisco Bay. Along 
with the swamp mallee other tough small trees adapted to our conditions are available. 
In the new Street Tree Master Plan, Eucalyptus and related trees are used sparingly, and in 
locations calling for their unique adaptations.

--- page 136 ---
Item #2  
From: Drew < > 
Date: April 1, 2022 at 16:56:12 EDT 
To: Matthew Fried <mfried@cityofsanmateo.org> 
Subject: San Mateo Street Tree Master Plan - one "bigger" feedback item plus a few misc 
 
Hello Matthew, 
 
I noticed the next Parks & Recreation Commission Meeting included the agenda item "San 
Mateo Street Tree Master Plan - Update". I read/scanned through the materials posted 
including the "San Mateo Street Tree Master Plan - Draft". 
 
First, this Draft document was great to read/scan through! I appreciated each section that 
discussed so many things I have wondered about. I plan to keep this document as a 
reference going forward. Thank you! 
 
My "bigger" feedback item, and thus my email, is I think there is a "section" missing. There 
is a section on "Site Appropriate Canopy Size and Shape" which is great because it talks 
about the above ground power lines. Also, the section on Preference for Evergreen Trees 
has comments about solar generation which is great too. These sections lead me to notice 
what is "missing" is a section on underground utilities. I think some write-up similar to the 
other sections would be useful. It would help educate that "clear space" above ground may 
not always be clear space to plant because many times along streets there are underground 
utilities. 
 
For example, if one looked at my house/lot, we have potential places to plant since the 
overhead lines are on the other side of both streets. (We have a corner lot.) But, below 
ground there is a water line on one side and a gas line on the other. The water line is about 
4' from the sidewalk/rolled curb and about 3' below grade. The gas line is about 2' from the 
sidewalk/rolled curb and maybe 3' below grade. While I would be game to plant trees, we 
don't feel comfortable planting them near these lines. 
 
Since I'm emailing, a few other smaller items of feedback: 1a) I think the notes on solar in 
the evergreen section could also be broken out into a section on "Sun and Solar energy 
needs". (Height/location of trees is definitely on my list when it comes to future solar 
panels.) This could lead to, on the street listings, to favor different heights on the 
north/south side of streets. Yes, it is another factor similar to the above ground power 
lines. 1b) The discussion on visual cohesion and views of the Bay, etc. might also lend itself 
to a combined "new" section with the solar energy piece that covers tree heights in context 
with some other specific, important needs. 
 
2) Wildfires - I think some specific mention on how trees can be incorporated into wildfire 
preparation plans would be useful. Perhaps, this can be a subsection under the "...Changing 
Climate Conditions" section. Perhaps, this can be a subsection, but specifically labeled so it 
can be listed in the Table of Contents. 
 
3) Some discussions on streets right-of-way space and trees could be useful especially 
considering the street listing later in the document. While there was some information 
136 of 157

--- page 137 ---
already included, I think perhaps some "typical" sections to help "ground" the discussion. 
For example, some streets have rolled curb/sidewalk setups so where are trees to be 
planted. Other streets have a cub, but perhaps only 2 feet of green strip before the 
sidewalk. Other areas have the tree cut out grates. There are streets listed in the later part 
of the report that I don't see how any trees could be planted given how things currently are 
setup/working. 
 
Again, thank you for doing this update/report! I did enjoy and appreciate reading/scanning 
through the document. 
 
Drew 
137 of 157

--- page 138 ---
Update to 
Street Tree Master Plan
Park and Recreation Commission
April 6, 2022
Matthew Fried, Managing Arborist
138 of 157

--- page 139 ---
Agenda
1. Background and current growing conditions
2. Impetus for vast revision to planting palette
3. Use of expert consultant 
4. Desired street tree attributes 
5. Expected outcomes
6. Growing pains and the pace of change 
7. Photos of some new introductions
8. Questions
139 of 157

--- page 140 ---
Background
•
Document specifies what trees to be planted as street trees in public right of way. 
•
Each block is assigned one or two species of trees as official street trees.
•
Most street trees are planted by City. Permit required for resident to plant in right of way.
•
Allows City to plan for composition of overall forest and long-term maintenance.
•
Managing Arborist may allow for deviation from official species in limited circumstances.
140 of 157

--- page 141 ---
Current Conditions
• Many deciduous trees imported from much wetter eastern U.S. (sycamore, magnolia, maples)
• Annual temperatures are increasing
• Annual precipitation is decreasing and becoming less predictable
• Water is becoming scarcer, and drought restrictions are increasing
• Trees under stress are more susceptible to pest/disease infestation > poor outcomes
141 of 157

--- page 142 ---
The Case for Vast Revisions
• Existing species palette not adapted to current climate conditions. Even less adapted to future 
conditions in coming decades.
• Increased susceptibility to pests/disease
• Decreased survival/longevity
• Species ranges are shifting north, so San Mateo must “look south” for climate-resilient species
• Adapted to hotter temperatures
• Lower water requirements
• Expert consultant, Dave Muffly (Oaktoptia, LLC)
142 of 157

--- page 143 ---
Use of Consultant (Oaktopia LLC)
• Dave Muffly (Oaktoptia, LLC)
• Consulting Arborist in Bay Area 1998-2009, 2018-Present
• Senior Arborist, Apple Inc. 2011-2018
• Oversaw production and install of 9,000 trees on Apple Park Cupertino campus
• Extensive plant selection experience, focus on drought-adapted species
• Oaktopia reassigned all 1,900 City blocks with climate-adapted species
• The following desired tree attributes and site conditions were considered in species selection…
143 of 157

--- page 144 ---
Desired Street Tree Attributes
• Proven drought tolerance and longevity. 
• Representative of broad genetic diversity. 
• Ability to withstand changing climate patterns. 
• Minimal debris production. 
• High resistance to pests and disease. 
• Low root damage potential. 
• Failure-resistant branch structure. 
• Site-appropriate canopy size and shape. 
• Nursery availability. 
144 of 157

--- page 145 ---
Expected Outcomes
• Higher survival, better plant vigor, lower mortality
• Fewer costly and unsightly pest issues
• Less broken and displaced sidewalks
• Less disruptive debris production (natural and pest-related)
• Fewer and less severe branch failures
• Green streetscape for greater portion of the year (evergreen bias)
145 of 157

--- page 146 ---
Growing Pains and the Pace of Change
• The Master Plan only informs the species of trees planted during normal operations. Trees will 
not be proactively identified for replacement.
• With 23,000 street trees and 200 “course of business” replacements a year, the composition of 
the urban forest could change by less than 1% annually.
• There will be a shift away from monoculture/uniformity as new species are planted.
146 of 157
